      <p>
       <b>
        Venetian
       </b>
       or
       <b>
        Venetan
       </b>
       (Venetian:
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         vèneto
        </span>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         vènet
        </span>
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         łéngua vèneta
        </span>
       </i>
       ) is a
       <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">
        Romance language
       </a>
       spoken as a native language by almost four million people in the northeast of Italy,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ethn_7-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ethn-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       mostly in the
       <a href="/wiki/Veneto" title="Veneto">
        Veneto
       </a>
       region of
       <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">
        Italy
       </a>
       , where most of the five million inhabitants can understand it. It is sometimes spoken and often well understood outside Veneto, in
       <a href="/wiki/Trentino" title="Trentino">
        Trentino
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Friuli" title="Friuli">
        Friuli
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Venezia_Giulia" title="Venezia Giulia">
        Venezia Giulia
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Istria" title="Istria">
        Istria
       </a>
       , and some towns of
       <a href="/wiki/Dalmatia" title="Dalmatia">
        Dalmatia
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">
        Slovenia
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">
        Croatia
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">
        Brazil
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">
        Argentina
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">
        Mexico
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Although referred to as an
       <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Italy" title="Languages of Italy">
        Italian dialect
       </a>
       (Venetian
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         diałeto
        </span>
       </i>
       , Italian
       <i>
        <span lang="it" xml:lang="it">
         dialetto
        </span>
       </i>
       ) even by its speakers, Venetian is a separate language. Its precise place within the Romance language family is controversial; see
       <a href="#Classification">
        below
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

          <span class="toctext">

          </span>
         </a>
        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-5">

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Venetian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Venetian_literature" title="Venetian literature">
        Venetian literature
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Like all Italian dialects in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romance_language" title="Romance language">
        Romance language
       </a>
       family, Venetian is descended from
       <a href="/wiki/Vulgar_Latin" title="Vulgar Latin">
        Vulgar Latin
       </a>
       and influenced by the
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">
        Italian language
       </a>
       . Venetian is attested as a written language in the 13th century. There are also influences and parallelisms with
       <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">
        Greek
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Albanian_language" title="Albanian language">
        Albanian
       </a>
       in words such as
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         pirón
        </span>
       </i>
       (fork),
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         inpiràr
        </span>
       </i>
       (to fork),
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         caréga
        </span>
       </i>
       (chair) and
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         fanèla
        </span>
       </i>
       (T-shirt).
      </p>
      <p>
       The language enjoyed substantial prestige in the days of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Venetian_Republic" title="Venetian Republic">
        Venetian Republic
       </a>
       , when it attained the status of a
       <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">
        lingua franca
       </a>
       in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediterranean" title="Mediterranean">
        Mediterranean
       </a>
       . Notable Venetian-language authors include the playwrights
       <a href="/wiki/Angelo_Beolco" title="Angelo Beolco">
        Ruzante
       </a>
       (1502–1542),
       <a href="/wiki/Carlo_Goldoni" title="Carlo Goldoni">
        Carlo Goldoni
       </a>
       (1707–1793) and
       <a href="/wiki/Carlo_Gozzi" title="Carlo Gozzi">
        Carlo Gozzi
       </a>
       (1720–1806). Following the old Italian theatre tradition (
       <i>
        <span lang="it" xml:lang="it">
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commedia_dell%27Arte" title="Commedia dell'Arte">
          Commedia dell'Arte
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       ), they used Venetian in their comedies as the speech of the common folk. They are ranked among the foremost Italian theatrical authors of all time, and plays by Goldoni and Gozzi are still performed today all over the world.
      </p>
      <p>
       Other notable works in Venetian are the translations of the
       <a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">
        Iliad
       </a>
       by
       <a href="/wiki/Giacomo_Casanova" title="Giacomo Casanova">
        Casanova
       </a>
       (1725–1798) and Francesco Boaretti, and the poems of
       <a href="/wiki/Biagio_Marin" title="Biagio Marin">
        Biagio Marin
       </a>
       (1891–1985). Notable too is a manuscript titled
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Dialogo_de_Cecco_di_Ronchitti_da_Bruzene_in_perpuosito_de_la_stella_Nuova" title="Dialogo de Cecco di Ronchitti da Bruzene in perpuosito de la stella Nuova">
         Dialogue of Cecco di Ronchitti of Brugine about the New Star
        </a>
       </i>
       attributed to Girolamo Spinelli, perhaps with some supervision by
       <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">
        Galileo Galilei
       </a>
       for scientific details.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       As a
       <a href="/wiki/Literary_language" title="Literary language">
        literary language
       </a>
       , Venetian was overshadowed by
       <a href="/wiki/Dante_Alighieri" title="Dante Alighieri">
        Dante
       </a>
       's
       <a href="/wiki/Tuscan_dialect" title="Tuscan dialect">
        Tuscan
       </a>
       "dialect" (the best known writers of the Renaissance, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Petrarch" title="Petrarch">
        Petrarch
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boccaccio" title="Boccaccio">
        Boccaccio
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Machiavelli" title="Machiavelli">
        Machiavelli
       </a>
       , were Tuscan and wrote in the Tuscan language) and
       <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_France" title="Languages of France">
        languages of France
       </a>
       like
       <a href="/wiki/Occitan_language" title="Occitan language">
        Occitan
       </a>
       and the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/O%C3%AFl_languages" title="Oïl languages">
        Oïl languages
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Even before the demise of the Republic, Venetian gradually ceased to be used for administrative purposes in favor of the Tuscan-derived Italian language that had been proposed and used as a vehicle for a common Italian culture, strongly supported by eminent Venetian humanists and poets, from
       <a href="/wiki/Pietro_Bembo" title="Pietro Bembo">
        Pietro Bembo
       </a>
       (1470–1547), a crucial figure in the development of the
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">
        Italian language
       </a>
       itself, to
       <a href="/wiki/Ugo_Foscolo" title="Ugo Foscolo">
        Ugo Foscolo
       </a>
       (1778–1827).
      </p>
      <p>
       Virtually all modern Venetian speakers are
       <a href="/wiki/Diglossia" title="Diglossia">
        diglossic
       </a>
       with Italian. The present situation raises questions about the language's medium term survival. Despite recent steps to recognize it, Venetian remains far below the threshold of inter-generational transfer with younger generations preferring standard Italian in many situations. The dilemma is further complicated by the ongoing large-scale arrival of immigrants, who only speak or learn standard Italian.
      </p>
      <p>
       Venetian spread to other continents as a result of mass migration from the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Veneto_region" title="Veneto region">
        Veneto region
       </a>
       between 1870 and 1905, and 1945 and 1960. This itself was a by-product of the 1866 annexation, because the latter subjected the poorest sectors of the population to the vagaries of a newly integrated, developing national industrial economy centered on north-western Italy. Tens of thousands of peasants and craftsmen were thrown off their lands or out of their workshops, forced to seek better fortune overseas.
      </p>
      <p>
       Venetian migrants created large Venetian-speaking communities in
       <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">
        Argentina
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">
        Brazil
       </a>
       (see
       <a href="/wiki/Talian_dialect" title="Talian dialect">
        Talian
       </a>
       ), and
       <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">
        Mexico
       </a>
       (see
       <a href="/wiki/Chipilo_Venetian_dialect" title="Chipilo Venetian dialect">
        Chipilo Venetian dialect
       </a>
       ), where the language is still spoken today. Internal migrations under the
       <a href="/wiki/Fascism" title="Fascism">
        Fascist
       </a>
       regime also sent many Venetian speakers to other regions of Italy, like southern
       <a href="/wiki/Lazio" title="Lazio">
        Lazio
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Currently, some firms have chosen to use the Venetian language in advertising as a famous beer did some years ago (
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         Xe foresto solo el nome
        </span>
       </i>
       , "only the name is foreign").
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Forum_Nathion_Veneta_9-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Forum_Nathion_Veneta-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In other cases advertisements in the Venice region are given a "Venetian flavour" by adding a Venetian word to standard Italian: for instance an airline used the verb
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         xe
        </span>
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         <b>
          Xe
         </b>
        </span>
        <span lang="it" xml:lang="it">
         sempre più grande
        </span>
       </i>
       , "it is always bigger") into an Italian sentence (the correct Venetian being
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         el xe senpre più grando
        </span>
       </i>
       )
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       to advertise new flights from
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marco_Polo_Airport" title="Marco Polo Airport">
        Marco Polo Airport
       </a>
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2007)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       On March 28, 2007, the Regional Council of Vèneto officially recognized the existence of the Venetian language (
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         Łéngua Vèneta
        </span>
       </i>
       ) by passing with an almost unanimous vote a law on the
       <i>
        <span lang="it" xml:lang="it">
         Tutela e valorizzazione della lingua e della cultura veneta
        </span>
       </i>
       ("Law on the Protection and Valorisation of the Venetian Language and Culture") with the vote of both governing and opposition parties.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic_distribution">
        Geographic distribution
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Venetian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Geographic distribution">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <div style="width:52px">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/>
          </a>
         </div>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text">
         <div class="mbox-text-span">
          This section
          <b>
           needs additional citations for
           <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
            verification
           </a>
          </b>
          .
          <span class="hide-when-compact">
           Please help
           <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Venetian_language&amp;action=edit">
            improve this article
           </a>
           by
           <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">
            adding citations to reliable sources
           </a>
           . Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.
          </span>
          <small>
           <i>
            (September 2015)
           </i>
          </small>
          <small class="hide-when-compact">
           <i>
            (
            <a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">
             Learn how and when to remove this template message
            </a>
            )
           </i>
          </small>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Venetian is spoken mainly in the Italian regions of
       <a href="/wiki/Veneto" title="Veneto">
        Veneto
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Friuli-Venezia_Giulia" title="Friuli-Venezia Giulia">
        Friuli-Venezia Giulia
       </a>
       and in both
       <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">
        Slovenia
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">
        Croatia
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Istria" title="Istria">
        Istria
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Dalmatia" title="Dalmatia">
        Dalmatia
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Kvarner_Gulf" title="Kvarner Gulf">
        Kvarner Gulf
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2010)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       Smaller communities are found in
       <a href="/wiki/Lombardy" title="Lombardy">
        Lombardy
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Trentino" title="Trentino">
        Trentino
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Emilia-Romagna" title="Emilia-Romagna">
        Emilia-Romagna
       </a>
       (in
       <a href="/wiki/Mantua" title="Mantua">
        Mantua
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Rimini" title="Rimini">
        Rimini
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Forl%C3%AC" title="Forlì">
        Forlì
       </a>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Sardinia" title="Sardinia">
        Sardinia
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Arborea" title="Arborea">
        Arborea
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Terralba" title="Terralba">
        Terralba
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Fertilia" title="Fertilia">
        Fertilia
       </a>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Lazio" title="Lazio">
        Lazio
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Pontine_Marshes" title="Pontine Marshes">
        Pontine Marshes
       </a>
       ), and formerly in
       <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">
        Romania
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Tulcea" title="Tulcea">
        Tulcea
       </a>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       It is also spoken in North and South America by the descendants of Italian immigrants. Notable examples of this are
       <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">
        Argentina
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">
        Brazil
       </a>
       , particularly the city of
       <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo" title="São Paulo">
        São Paulo
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Talian_dialect" title="Talian dialect">
        Talian dialect
       </a>
       spoken in the
       <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">
        Brazilian
       </a>
       states of
       <a href="/wiki/Esp%C3%ADrito_Santo" title="Espírito Santo">
        Espírito Santo
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo" title="São Paulo">
        São Paulo
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Paran%C3%A1_(state)" title="Paraná (state)">
        Paraná
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Rio_Grande_do_Sul" title="Rio Grande do Sul">
        Rio Grande do Sul
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Santa_Catarina_(state)" title="Santa Catarina (state)">
        Santa Catarina
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">
        Mexico
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Chipilo_Venetian_dialect" title="Chipilo Venetian dialect">
        Chipilo Venetian dialect
       </a>
       is spoken in the state of
       <a href="/wiki/Puebla" title="Puebla">
        Puebla
       </a>
       and the town of
       <a href="/wiki/Chipilo" title="Chipilo">
        Chipilo
       </a>
       . The town was settled by immigrants from the
       <a href="/wiki/Veneto" title="Veneto">
        Veneto
       </a>
       region, and some of their descendants have preserved the language to this day. People from Chipilo have gone on to make satellite colonies in Mexico, especially in the states of
       <a href="/wiki/Guanajuato" title="Guanajuato">
        Guanajuato
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Quer%C3%A9taro" title="Querétaro">
        Querétaro
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/State_of_Mexico" title="State of Mexico">
        State of Mexico
       </a>
       . Venetian has also survived in the state of
       <a href="/wiki/Veracruz" title="Veracruz">
        Veracruz
       </a>
       , where other Italian migrants have settled from the late 1800s. The people of Chipilo preserve their dialect and call it
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         chipileño
        </span>
       </i>
       and it has been preserved as a variant since the 19th century. The variant of the Venetan language spoken by the
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         Cipiłàn
        </span>
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        <span lang="es" xml:lang="es">
         Chipileños
        </span>
       </i>
       ) is northern Trevisàn-Feltrìn-Belumàt.
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2009, the Brazilian city of
       <a href="/wiki/Serafina_Corr%C3%AAa" title="Serafina Corrêa">
        Serafina Corrêa
       </a>
       , in the state of Rio Grande do Sul, gave
       <a href="/wiki/Talian_dialect" title="Talian dialect">
        Talian
       </a>
       a joint official status alongside
       <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">
        Portuguese
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Until the middle of the 20th century, Venetian was also spoken on the Greek Island of
       <a href="/wiki/Corfu" title="Corfu">
        Corfu
       </a>
       , which had long been under the rule of the
       <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">
        Republic of Venice
       </a>
       . Moreover, Venetian had been adopted by a large proportion of the population of
       <a href="/wiki/Cephalonia" title="Cephalonia">
        Cephalonia
       </a>
       , one of the
       <a href="/wiki/Ionian_Islands" title="Ionian Islands">
        Ionian Islands
       </a>
       , because the island was part of the
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         <a href="/wiki/Stato_da_M%C3%A0r" title="Stato da Màr">
          Stato da Màr
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       for almost three centuries.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">
        Classification
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Venetian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Classification">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Venetian is a Romance language and thus descends from
       <a href="/wiki/Vulgar_Latin" title="Vulgar Latin">
        Vulgar Latin
       </a>
       . According to Tagliavini, it is one of the
       <a href="/wiki/Italo-Dalmatian_languages" title="Italo-Dalmatian languages">
        Italo-Dalmatian languages
       </a>
       and most closely related to
       <a href="/wiki/Istriot_language" title="Istriot language">
        Istriot
       </a>
       on the one hand and Tuscan–Italian on the other.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Some authors include it among the
       <a href="/wiki/Gallo-Italic_languages" title="Gallo-Italic languages">
        Gallo-Italic languages
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CAT_15-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-CAT-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but by most authors, it is treated as separate.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Renzi_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Renzi-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Typologically, Venetian has little in common with the Gallo-Italic languages of northwestern Italy, but shows some affinity to nearby Istriot.
      </p>
      <p>
       Despite the language region being surrounded by
       <a href="/wiki/Gallo-Italic_languages" title="Gallo-Italic languages">
        Gallo-Italic languages
       </a>
       , Venetian does not share traits with these immediate neighbors. Scholars stress Venetian's characteristic lack of Gallo-Italic traits (
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         agallicità
        </span>
       </i>
       )
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       or traits found further afield in
       <a href="/wiki/Gallo-Romance_languages" title="Gallo-Romance languages">
        Gallo-Romance languages
       </a>
       (e.g.
       <a href="/wiki/Occitan_language" title="Occitan language">
        Occitan
       </a>
       , French,
       <a href="/wiki/Franco-Proven%C3%A7al_language" title="Franco-Provençal language">
        Franco-Provençal
       </a>
       )
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       or the
       <a href="/wiki/Rhaeto-Romance_languages" title="Rhaeto-Romance languages">
        Rhaeto-Romance languages
       </a>
       (e.g.
       <a href="/wiki/Friulian_language" title="Friulian language">
        Friulian
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Romansh_language" title="Romansh language">
        Romansh
       </a>
       ). For example, Venetian did not undergo vowel rounding or nasalization, palatalize
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /kt/
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ks/
       </span>
       , or develop rising diphthongs
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ei/
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ou/
       </span>
       , and it preserved final syllables, whereas, like in
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">
        Italian
       </a>
       , Venetian diphthongization occurs in historically open syllables.
      </p>
      <p>
       Modern Venetian is not a close relative of the
       <a href="/wiki/Extinct_language" title="Extinct language">
        extinct
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Venetic_language" title="Venetic language">
        Venetic language
       </a>
       spoken in the Veneto region before Roman expansion, although both are
       <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">
        Indo-European
       </a>
       , and Venetic may have been an
       <a href="/wiki/Italic_languages" title="Italic languages">
        Italic
       </a>
       language, like
       <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
        Latin
       </a>
       , the ancestor of Venetian and most other
       <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Italy" title="Languages of Italy">
        languages of Italy
       </a>
       . The earlier Venetic people gave their name to the city and region, which is why the modern language has a similar name.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_variants">
        Regional variants
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Venetian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Regional variants">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The main regional varieties and subvarieties of Venetian are
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         Central
        </i>
        (
        <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Padua" title="Province of Padua">
         Padua
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Vicenza" title="Province of Vicenza">
         Vicenza
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Rovigo" title="Province of Rovigo">
         Polesine
        </a>
        ), with about 1,500,000 speakers.
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Eastern/Coastal
        </i>
        (
        <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Venice" title="Province of Venice">
         Venice
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Triestine_dialect" title="Triestine dialect">
         Trieste
        </a>
        (see),
        <a href="/wiki/Grado,_Friuli-Venezia_Giulia" title="Grado, Friuli-Venezia Giulia">
         Grado
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Istria" title="Istria">
         Istria
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Fiuman_dialect" title="Fiuman dialect">
         Fiume
        </a>
        ).
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Western
        </i>
        (
        <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Verona" title="Province of Verona">
         Verona
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Trentino" title="Trentino">
         Trentino
        </a>
        ).
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         North-Central
        </i>
        <i>
         <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
          Destra Piave
         </span>
        </i>
        of the
        <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Treviso" title="Province of Treviso">
         Province of Treviso
        </a>
        , most of the
        <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Pordenone" title="Province of Pordenone">
         Province of Pordenone
        </a>
        ).
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Northern
        </i>
        <i>
         <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
          Sinistra Piave
         </span>
        </i>
        of the Province of Treviso, (
        <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Belluno" title="Province of Belluno">
         Belluno
        </a>
        , comprising
        <a href="/wiki/Feltre" title="Feltre">
         Feltre
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Agordo" title="Agordo">
         Agordo
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Cadore" title="Cadore">
         Cadore
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Zoldo_Alto" title="Zoldo Alto">
         Zoldo Alto
        </a>
        ).
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       All these variants are mutually intelligible, with a minimum 92% between the most diverging ones (Central and Western). Modern speakers reportedly can still understand Venetian texts from the 14th century to some extent.
      </p>
      <p>
       Other noteworthy variants are:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        the variety spoken in
        <a href="/wiki/Chioggia" title="Chioggia">
         Chioggia
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        the variety spoken in the
        <a href="/wiki/Pontine_Marshes" title="Pontine Marshes">
         Pontine Marshes
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        the variety spoken in
        <a href="/wiki/Dalmatia" title="Dalmatia">
         Dalmatia
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        the
        <a href="/wiki/Talian_dialect" title="Talian dialect">
         Talian dialect
        </a>
        of
        <a href="/wiki/Ant%C3%B4nio_Prado" title="Antônio Prado">
         Antônio Prado
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Entre_Rios,_Santa_Catarina" title="Entre Rios, Santa Catarina">
         Entre Rios, Santa Catarina
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Toledo,_Paran%C3%A1" title="Toledo, Paraná">
         Toledo, Paraná
        </a>
        , among other southern
        <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">
         Brazilian
        </a>
        cities.
       </li>
       <li>
        the
        <a href="/wiki/Chipilo_Venetian_dialect" title="Chipilo Venetian dialect">
         Chipilo Venetian dialect
        </a>
        (
        <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
         Spanish
        </a>
        :
        <i>
         <span lang="es" xml:lang="es">
          Chipileño
         </span>
        </i>
        ) of
        <a href="/wiki/Chipilo" title="Chipilo">
         Chipilo
        </a>
        , Mexico
       </li>
       <li>
        Peripheral
        <a href="/wiki/Creole_language" title="Creole language">
         creole languages
        </a>
        along the southern border (nearly extinct).
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">
        Grammar
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Venetian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Grammar">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Venetian_grammar" title="Venetian grammar">
        Venetian grammar
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Calle_berlendis,_Venice.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1932" data-file-width="2576" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Calle_berlendis%2C_Venice.jpg/200px-Calle_berlendis%2C_Venice.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Calle_berlendis%2C_Venice.jpg/300px-Calle_berlendis%2C_Venice.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Calle_berlendis%2C_Venice.jpg/400px-Calle_berlendis%2C_Venice.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Calle_berlendis,_Venice.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A street sign (
         <i>
          <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
           nizioléto
          </span>
         </i>
         ) in Venice using the Venetian
         <i>
          <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
           calle
          </span>
         </i>
         , as opposed to the Italian
         <i>
          <span lang="it" xml:lang="it">
           via
          </span>
         </i>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Inschrift_Venezianerhaus.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2808" data-file-width="2839" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Inschrift_Venezianerhaus.JPG/200px-Inschrift_Venezianerhaus.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Inschrift_Venezianerhaus.JPG/300px-Inschrift_Venezianerhaus.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Inschrift_Venezianerhaus.JPG/400px-Inschrift_Venezianerhaus.JPG 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Inschrift_Venezianerhaus.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <i>
          <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
           Lasa pur dir
          </span>
         </i>
         (Let them speak), an inscription on the Venetian House in
         <a href="/wiki/Piran" title="Piran">
          Piran
         </a>
         , southwestern Slovenia.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Like most Romance languages, Venetian has mostly abandoned the Latin
       <a href="/wiki/Declension" title="Declension">
        case system
       </a>
       , in favor of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Preposition" title="Preposition">
        prepositions
       </a>
       and a more rigid
       <a href="/wiki/Subject%E2%80%93verb%E2%80%93object" title="Subject–verb–object">
        subject–verb–object
       </a>
       sentence structure. It has thus become more
       <a href="/wiki/Analytic_language" title="Analytic language">
        analytic
       </a>
       , if not quite as much as English. Venetian also has the Romance
       <a href="/wiki/Article_(grammar)" title="Article (grammar)">
        articles
       </a>
       , both definite (derived from the Latin demonstrative
       <i>
        <span lang="la" xml:lang="la">
         ille
        </span>
       </i>
       ) and indefinite (derived from the numeral
       <i>
        <span lang="la" xml:lang="la">
         unus
        </span>
       </i>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       Venetian also retained the Latin concepts of
       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">
        gender
       </a>
       (masculine and feminine) and
       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_number" title="Grammatical number">
        number
       </a>
       (singular and plural). Unlike the Gallo-Iberian languages, which form plurals by adding
       <i>
        -s
       </i>
       , Venetian forms plurals in a manner similar to standard Italian. Nouns and adjectives can be modified by suffixes that indicate several qualities such as size, endearment, deprecation, etc. Adjectives (usually postfixed) and articles are inflected to agree with the noun in gender and number, but it is important to mention that the suffix might be deleted because the article is the part that suggests the number. However, Italian is influencing the Venetian language:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
          el gato graso
         </span>
        </i>
        , the fat (male) cat.
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
          ła gata grasa
         </span>
        </i>
        , the fat (female) cat.
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
          i gati grasi
         </span>
        </i>
        , the fat (male) cats.
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
          łe gate grase
         </span>
        </i>
        , the fat (female) cats.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       In conservative Venetian, the article alone may convey the gender:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
          i gat gras
         </span>
        </i>
        , the fat (all males or males and females) cats.
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
          łe gat gras
         </span>
        </i>
        , the fat (female) cats.
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
          el gatòn graso
         </span>
        </i>
        , the fat big (male) cat.
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
          ła gatòna grasa
         </span>
        </i>
        , the fat big (female) cat.
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
          un bel gateło
         </span>
        </i>
        , a nice small (male) cat.
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
          na beła gateła
         </span>
        </i>
        , a nice small (female) cat.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       No native Venetic words seem to have survived in present Venetian, but there may be some traces left in the morphology, such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Morpheme" title="Morpheme">
        morpheme
       </a>
       -
       <i>
        esto
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        asto
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        isto
       </i>
       for the past participle, which can be found in Venetic inscriptions from about 500 BC:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <b>
         Venetian
        </b>
        :
        <i>
         <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
          Mi go fazesto
         </span>
        </i>
        ("I have done")
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Venetian Italian
        </b>
        :
        <i>
         <span lang="it" xml:lang="it">
          Mi go fato
         </span>
        </i>
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Standard Italian
        </b>
        :
        <i>
         <span lang="it" xml:lang="it">
          Io ho fatto
         </span>
        </i>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Redundant_subject_pronouns">
        Redundant subject pronouns
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Venetian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Redundant subject pronouns">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       A peculiarity of Venetian grammar is a "semi-analytical" verbal flexion, with a compulsory "
       <a href="/wiki/Clitic" title="Clitic">
        clitic
       </a>
       subject pronoun" before the verb in many sentences, "echoing" the subject as an ending or a weak pronoun. Independent/emphatic pronouns (e.g.
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         ti
        </span>
       </i>
       ), on the contrary, are optional. The clitic subject pronoun (
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         te, el/ła, i/łe
        </span>
       </i>
       ) is used with the 2nd and 3rd person singular, and with the 3rd person plural. This feature may have arisen as a compensation for the fact that the 2nd- and 3rd-person inflections for most verbs, which are still distinct in Italian and many other Romance languages, are identical in Venetian.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Piedmontese_language" title="Piedmontese language">
        Piedmontese language
       </a>
       also has clitic subject pronouns, but the rules are somewhat different. The function of clitics is particularly visible in long sentences, which do not always have clear intonational breaks to easily tell apart vocative and imperative in sharp commands from exclamations with "shouted indicative". For instance, in Venetian the clitic
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         el
        </span>
       </i>
       marks the indicative verb and its masculine singular subject, otherwise there is an imperative preceded by a vocative. Although some grammars regard these clitics as "redundant", they actually provide specific additional information as they mark number and gender, thus providing number-/gender- agreement between the subject(s) and the verb, which does not necessarily show this information on its endings.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Interrogative_inflection">
        Interrogative inflection
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Venetian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Interrogative inflection">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Venetian also has a special
       <i>
        interrogative
       </i>
       verbal flexion used for direct questions, which also incorporates a redundant pronoun:
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>
         Italian
        </th>
        <th>
         Venetian
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          <span lang="it" xml:lang="it">
           (Tu) eri sporco?
          </span>
         </i>
         <br/>
         ("Were you dirty?")
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
           (Ti) jèristu onto?
          </span>
         </i>
         <br/>
         or
         <i>
          <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
           (Ti) xèrito spazo?
          </span>
         </i>
         <br/>
         (lit. "You were-you dirty?")
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          <span lang="it" xml:lang="it">
           Il cane era sporco?
          </span>
         </i>
         <br/>
         ("Was the dog dirty?")
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
           El can jèreło onto?
          </span>
         </i>
         <br/>
         (lit. "The dog was-he dirty?")
         <br/>
         or
         <i>
          <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
           Jèreło onto el can ?
          </span>
         </i>
         <br/>
         (lit. "Was-he dirty the dog ?")
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          <span lang="it" xml:lang="it">
           (Tu) ti sei domandato?
          </span>
         </i>
         <br/>
         ("Have you asked yourself?")
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
           (Ti) te seto domandà?
          </span>
         </i>
         <br/>
         (lit. "You to-yourself have-you asked?")
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Auxiliary_verbs">
        Auxiliary verbs
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Venetian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Auxiliary verbs">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Reflexive tenses use the auxiliary verb
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         avér
        </span>
       </i>
       ("to have"), as in English,
       <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">
        German
       </a>
       , and Spanish; instead of
       <i>
        <span lang="it" xml:lang="it">
         èssar
        </span>
       </i>
       ("to be"), which would be normal in Italian. The past participle is invariable, unlike Italian:
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>
         Italian
        </th>
        <th>
         Venetian
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          <span lang="it" xml:lang="it">
           (Tu) ti sei lavato
          </span>
         </i>
         <br/>
         (lit. "(You) yourself are washed")
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
           (Ti) te te à/gà/ghè lavà
          </span>
         </i>
         <br/>
         (lit. "(You) you yourself have washed")
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          <span lang="it" xml:lang="it">
           (Loro) si sono svegliati
          </span>
         </i>
         <br/>
         (lit. "(They) themselves are awakened")
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
           (Lori) i se gà/à svejà
          </span>
         </i>
         <br/>
         (lit. "(They) they themselves have awakened")
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Continuing_action">
        Continuing action
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Venetian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Continuing action">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Another peculiarity of the language is the use of the phrase
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         eser drìo
        </span>
       </i>
       (literally, "to be behind") to indicate continuing action:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Italian:
        <i>
         <span lang="it" xml:lang="it">
          Mio padre sta parlando
         </span>
        </i>
        ("My father is speaking").
       </li>
       <li>
        Venetian:
        <i>
         <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
          Mé pare 'l xe drìo(invià) parlàr
         </span>
        </i>
        (lit. "My father he is busy speaking").
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Indeed, the word
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         drio
        </span>
       </i>
       ("busy" or "engaged") also appears in other sentences:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Venetian:
        <i>
         <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
          So' drio far i mistieri
         </span>
        </i>
        lit. means "I am busy doing the housework" ("I'm doing it")
       </li>
       <li>
        Venetian:
        <i>
         <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
          Vo drio i mistieri
         </span>
        </i>
        lit. means "I go busy with the housework" ("I'm going to do it")
       </li>
       <li>
        Venetian:
        <i>
         <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
          Mé pare l'è in leto drio (invià) dormir
         </span>
        </i>
        lit. means "My father is in bed, busy sleeping" ("My father is sleeping in bed")
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Another progressive form uses the construction
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         essar là che
        </span>
       </i>
       (lit. "to be there that"):
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Venetian:
        <i>
         <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
          Me pàre 'l è là che 'l parla
         </span>
        </i>
        (lit. "My father he is there that he speaks").
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       The use of progressive tenses is more pervasive than in Italian; E.g.
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        English: "He wouldn't possibly have been speaking to you".
       </li>
       <li>
        Venetian:
        <i>
         <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
          No 'l sarìa mìa stat/stà drìo parlarte
         </span>
        </i>
        (lit. "Not-he would possibly have been behind to speak-to-you").
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       That construction does not occur in Italian:
       <i>
        *Non sarebbe mica stato parlandoti
       </i>
       is not syntactically valid.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Subordinate_clauses">
        Subordinate clauses
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Venetian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Subordinate clauses">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Subordinate clauses have double introduction ("whom that", "when that", "which that", "how that"), as in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_English_language" title="Old English language">
        Old English
       </a>
       :
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Italian:
        <i>
         <span lang="it" xml:lang="it">
          So di chi parli
         </span>
        </i>
        ("(I) know about whom (you) speak").
       </li>
       <li>
        Venetian:
        <i>
         <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
          So de chi che te parla
         </span>
        </i>
        (lit. "(I) know about whom that you-speak").
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       As in other Romance languages, the
       <a href="/wiki/Subjunctive_mood" title="Subjunctive mood">
        subjunctive mood
       </a>
       is widely used in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subordinate_clause" title="Subordinate clause">
        subordinate clauses
       </a>
       (although not always). Remarkably, although the use of subjunctive is weakening in many colloquial varieties of Italian, the Venetian subjunctive seems to be more resisting. For example, many Italian speakers often hesitate between subjunctive
       <i>
        <span lang="it" xml:lang="it">
         che fosse
        </span>
       </i>
       "that...were" and indicative
       <i>
        <span lang="it" xml:lang="it">
         che era
        </span>
       </i>
       "that...was" (though this phenomenon is generally sanctioned in the standard form), whereas almost no Venetian speaker would use the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indicative" title="Indicative">
        indicative
       </a>
       in the following examples. Notice that it is hardly possible to distinguish a colloquial and a standard form, Venetian being used especially in the spoken form.
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Standard Italian:
        <i>
         <span lang="it" xml:lang="it">
          Credevo che fosse...
         </span>
        </i>
        ("I thought that he were...")
       </li>
       <li>
        Colloquial Ital.:
        <i>
         <span lang="it" xml:lang="it">
          Credevo che era...
         </span>
        </i>
        ("I thought that he was...")
       </li>
       <li>
        Venetian:
        <i>
         <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
          Credéa/évo che 'l fuse...
         </span>
        </i>
        ("I thought that he were...")
       </li>
       <li>
        Venetian:
        <i>
         <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
          Credéa/évo che 'l
         </span>
         *xera...
        </i>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       For the same reasons, although Italian speakers may accept both
       <i>
        <span lang="it" xml:lang="it">
         vada
        </span>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <span lang="it" xml:lang="it">
         vado
        </span>
       </i>
       'I go-subj/indic.' in the colloquial style, nearly everybody would reject the Venetian indicative
       <i>
        *vo
       </i>
       in the following context.
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Standard Italian:
        <i>
         <span lang="it" xml:lang="it">
          E' meglio che vada
         </span>
        </i>
        ("I'd better go", lit. "it is better that I go" subj.)
       </li>
       <li>
        Standard Italian:
        <i>
         <span lang="it" xml:lang="it">
          E' meglio che vado
         </span>
        </i>
        ("I'd better go", lit. "it is better that I go" indic.)
       </li>
       <li>
        Venetian:
        <i>
         <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
          Xe mejo che vaga/vae
         </span>
        </i>
        ("I'd better go"-subj.)
       </li>
       <li>
        Venetian:
        <i>
         <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
          Xe mejo che
         </span>
         *vo
        </i>
       </li>
      </ul>

       <span class="mw-editsection">

        <td>

          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_flap" title="Alveolar flap">

         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Some dialects of Venetian have certain sounds not present in Italian, such as the interdental voiceless fricative
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [
        <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_dental_fricative" title="Voiceless dental fricative">
         θ
        </a>
        ]
       </span>
       , often spelled with ⟨ç⟩, ⟨z⟩, ⟨zh⟩, or ⟨ž⟩, and similar to
       <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">
        English
       </a>
       <i>
        th
       </i>
       in
       <i>
        thing
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        thought
       </i>
       . This sound occurs, for example, in
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         çéna
        </span>
       </i>
       ("supper", also written
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         zhena, žena
        </span>
       </i>
       ), which is pronounced the same as Castilian Spanish
       <i>
        <span lang="es" xml:lang="es">
         cena
        </span>
       </i>
       (which has the same meaning). The voiceless interdental fricative occurs in Bellunese, north-Trevisan, and in some Central Venetian rural areas around Padua, Vicenza and the mouth of the
       <a href="/wiki/Po_(river)" title="Po (river)">
        river Po
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Because the pronunciation variant
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [
        <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_dental_fricative" title="Voiceless dental fricative">
         θ
        </a>
        ]
       </span>
       is more typical of older speakers and speakers living outside of major cities, it has come to be socially stigmatized, and most speakers now use
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [
        <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_fricative#Voiceless_alveolar_sibilant" title="Voiceless alveolar fricative">
         s
        </a>
        ]
       </span>
       or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_sibilant_affricate" title="Voiceless alveolar sibilant affricate">
         ts
        </a>
        ]
       </span>
       instead of
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [
        <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_dental_fricative" title="Voiceless dental fricative">
         θ
        </a>
        ]
       </span>
       . In those dialects with the pronunciation
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [
        <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_fricative#Voiceless_alveolar_sibilant" title="Voiceless alveolar fricative">
         s
        </a>
        ]
       </span>
       , the sound has fallen together with ordinary ⟨s⟩, and so it is not uncommon to simply write ⟨s⟩ (or ⟨ss⟩ between vowels) instead of ⟨ç⟩ or ⟨zh⟩ (such as
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         sena
        </span>
       </i>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       Similarly some dialects of Venetian also have a voiced interdental fricative, often written ⟨z⟩ (as in
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         el pianze
        </span>
       </i>
       'he cries'); but in most dialects this sound is now pronounced either as
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiced_alveolar_sibilant_affricate" title="Voiced alveolar sibilant affricate">
         dz
        </a>
        ]
       </span>
       (Italian
       <i>
        voiced-Z
       </i>
       ), or more typically as
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [
        <a href="/wiki/Voiced_alveolar_fricative#Voiced_alveolar_sibilant" title="Voiced alveolar fricative">
         z
        </a>
        ]
       </span>
       (Italian
       <i>
        voiced-S
       </i>
       , written ⟨x⟩, as in
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         el pianxe
        </span>
       </i>
       ); in a few dialects the sound appears as
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [
        <a href="/wiki/Voiced_dental_and_alveolar_stops#Alveolar" title="Voiced dental and alveolar stops">
         d
        </a>
        ]
       </span>
       and may therefore be written instead with the letter ⟨d⟩, as in
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         el piande
        </span>
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Some varieties of Venetian also distinguish an ordinary
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_lateral_approximant" title="Alveolar lateral approximant">
         l
        </a>
        ]
       </span>
       vs. a weakened or
       <a href="/wiki/Lenition" title="Lenition">
        lenited
       </a>
       ("evanescent") ⟨l⟩, which in some orthographic norms is indicated with the letter ⟨ł⟩; in more conservative dialects, however, both ⟨l⟩ and ⟨ł⟩ are merged as ordinary
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_lateral_approximant" title="Alveolar lateral approximant">
         l
        </a>
        ]
       </span>
       . In those dialects that have both types, the precise phonetic realization of ⟨ł⟩ depends both on its phonological environment and on the dialect of the speaker. Typical realizations in the region of Venice include a voiced velar approximant or glide
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [
        <a href="/wiki/Voiced_velar_approximant" title="Voiced velar approximant">
         ɰ
        </a>
        ]
       </span>
       (usually described as nearly like an "e" and so often spelled as ⟨e⟩), when ⟨ł⟩ is adjacent (only) to back vowels (⟨a o u⟩), vs. a null realization when ⟨ł⟩ is adjacent to a front vowel (⟨i e⟩).
      </p>
      <p>
       In dialects further inland ⟨ł⟩ may be realized as a partially vocalised ⟨l⟩. Thus, for example,
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         góndoła
        </span>
       </i>
       'gondola' may sound like
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         góndoea
        </span>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         góndola
        </span>
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         góndoa
        </span>
       </i>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Venetian" title="Help:IPA/Venetian">
         [ˈɡondoɰa]
        </a>
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Venetian" title="Help:IPA/Venetian">
         [ˈɡondola]
        </a>
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Venetian" title="Help:IPA/Venetian">
         [ˈɡondoa]
        </a>
       </span>
       . In dialects having a null realization of intervocalic ⟨ł⟩, although pairs of words such as
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         scóła
        </span>
       </i>
       , "school" and
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         scóa
        </span>
       </i>
       , "broom" are
       <a href="/wiki/Homophone" title="Homophone">
        homophonous
       </a>
       (both being pronounced
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Venetian" title="Help:IPA/Venetian">
         [ˈskoa]
        </a>
       </span>
       ), they are still distinguished orthographically.
      </p>
      <p>
       Venetian, like Spanish, does not have the
       <a href="/wiki/Gemination" title="Gemination">
        geminate consonants
       </a>
       characteristic of standard Italian, Tuscan, Neapolitan and other languages of southern Italy; thus Italian
       <i>
        <span lang="it" xml:lang="it">
         fette
        </span>
       </i>
       ("slices"),
       <i>
        <span lang="it" xml:lang="it">
         palla
        </span>
       </i>
       ("ball") and
       <i>
        <span lang="it" xml:lang="it">
         penna
        </span>
       </i>
       ("pen") correspond to
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         féte
        </span>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         bała
        </span>
       </i>
       , and
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         péna
        </span>
       </i>
       in Venetian. The masculine singular noun ending, corresponding to
       <i>
        -o/-e
       </i>
       in Italian, is often unpronounced in Venetian, particularly in rural varieties: Italian
       <i>
        <span lang="it" xml:lang="it">
         pieno
        </span>
       </i>
       ("full") corresponds to Venetian
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         pien
        </span>
       </i>
       , Italian
       <i>
        <span lang="it" xml:lang="it">
         altare
        </span>
       </i>
       to Venetian
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         altar
        </span>
       </i>
       . The extent to which final vowels are deleted varies by dialect: the central–southern varieties delete vowels only after
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /
        <a href="/wiki/Dental,_alveolar_and_postalveolar_nasals#Alveolar" title="Dental, alveolar and postalveolar nasals">
         n
        </a>
        /
       </span>
       , whereas the northern variety delete vowels also after dental stops and velars; the eastern and western varieties are in between these two extremes.
      </p>
      <p>
       The velar nasal
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [
        <a href="/wiki/Velar_nasal" title="Velar nasal">
         ŋ
        </a>
        ]
       </span>
       (the final sound in English "song") occurs frequently in Venetian. A word-final
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /
        <a href="/wiki/Dental,_alveolar_and_postalveolar_nasals#Alveolar" title="Dental, alveolar and postalveolar nasals">
         n
        </a>
        /
       </span>
       is always velarized, which is especially obvious in the pronunciation of many local Venetian surnames that end in ⟨n⟩, such as
       <i>
        Mari
        <b>
         n
        </b>
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Venetian" title="Help:IPA/Venetian">
         [maˈɾiŋ]
        </a>
       </span>
       and
       <i>
        Mani
        <b>
         n
        </b>
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Venetian" title="Help:IPA/Venetian">
         [maˈniŋ]
        </a>
       </span>
       , as well as in common Venetian words such as
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         ma
         <b>
          n
         </b>
        </span>
       </i>
       (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Venetian" title="Help:IPA/Venetian">
         [ˈmaŋ]
        </a>
       </span>
       "hand"),
       <i>
        <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">
         piro
         <b>
          n
         </b>
        </span>
       </i>
       (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Venetian" title="Help:IPA/Venetian">
         [piˈɾoŋ]
        </a>
       </span>
       , "fork"). Moreover, Venetian always uses
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [
        <a href="/wiki/Velar_nasal" title="Velar nasal">
         ŋ
        </a>
        ]
       </span>
       in consonant clusters that start with a nasal, whereas Italian only uses
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [
        <a href="/wiki/Velar_nasal" title="Velar nasal">
         ŋ
        </a>
        ]
       </span>
       before velar stops: e.g.
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Venetian" title="Help:IPA/Venetian">
         [kaŋˈtaɾ]
        </a>
       </span>
       "to sing",
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Venetian" title="Help:IPA/Venetian">
         [iŋˈvɛɾno]
        </a>
       </span>
       "winter",
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Venetian" title="Help:IPA/Venetian">
         [ˈoŋzaɾ]
        </a>
       </span>
       "to anoint",
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Venetian" title="Help:IPA/Venetian">
         [ɾaŋˈd͡ʒaɾse]
        </a>
       </span>
       "to cope with".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zamboni_19-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-zamboni-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Speakers of Italian generally lack this sound and usually substitute a dental
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [
        <a href="/wiki/Dental,_alveolar_and_postalveolar_nasals#Alveolar" title="Dental, alveolar and postalveolar nasals">
         n
        </a>
        ]
       </span>
       for final Venetian
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [
        <a href="/wiki/Velar_nasal" title="Velar nasal">
         ŋ
        </a>
        ]
       </span>
       , changing for example
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Venetian" title="Help:IPA/Venetian">
         [maˈniŋ]
        </a>
       </span>
       to
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Italian" title="Help:IPA/Italian">
         [maˈnin̪ː]
        </a>
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Venetian" title="Help:IPA/Venetian">
         [maˈɾiŋ]
        </a>
       </span>
       to
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Italian" title="Help:IPA/Italian">
         [maˈrin̪ː]
        </a>
       </span>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Sample_etymological_lexicon">
        Sample etymological lexicon
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Venetian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Sample etymological lexicon">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       As a direct descent of regional spoken Latin, the Venetian lexicon derives its vocabulary substantially from Latin and (in more recent times) from Tuscan, so that most of its words are cognate with the corresponding words of Italian. Venetian includes however many words derived from other sources (such as Greek, Gothic, and German) that are not cognate with their equivalent words in Italian, such as:
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>
         Venetian
        </th>

        </th>
       </tr>

          </span>
         </i>
        </td>

        </td>

         <i lang="la" xml:lang="la">

         <i lang="la" xml:lang="la">

        </td>

       <tr>

        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          <span lang="vec" xml:lang="vec">

          </span>
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         lizard
        </td>
        <td>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Spelling_systems">
        Spelling systems
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Venetian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Spelling systems">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <div style="width:52px">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/>
          </a>
